  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer3764700344090415766">In <i>Time's Arrow</i> the doctor Tod T. Friendly dies and then feels markedly better, breaks up with his lovers as a prelude to seducing them, and mangles his patients before he sends them home. And all the while Tod's life races backward in time toward the one appalling moment in modern history when such reversals make sense.<br/><br/></span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/871103-i-would-like-to-know-the-context-of-the">i would like to know the context of the book.?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_871103'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F23031.Time_s_Arrow">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/871103-i-would-like-to-know-the-context-of-the">4 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/871103-i-would-like-to-know-the-context-of-the">Add your answer</a>
</li>

</div>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/60920099-kimw"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_100x100-259587f1619f5253426a4fa6fb508831.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/60920099-kimw">KimW</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Do you mean content? or Context? Those are two different things.<br/><br/>If you mean "content", like "what is it about" - it's about a man who's died right at <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Do you mean content? or Context? Those are two different things.<br /><br />If you mean "content", like "what is it about" - it's about a man who's died right at the start - but then comes back to life again, except he's living inside the head of another person. And what's more - time is moving backwards. So it starts with the main character watching this other person "un-die", and then sees the part where he goes to the hospital for a heart attack, and then sees him as an old man puttering around his house....all the way back through this other person's life until the day of his birth.<br /><br />It takes a little while for our main character to figure things out, but it makes him see everything in a really different way (with things going backwards, things like shopping or cooking and eating seem really strange), but you still can understand what's going on.<br /><br />The real twist - which I won't reveal - is when you find out some things about the person you're watching live their life backward.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerFooter'>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_858330333" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/858330333" />
    <a title="Violet wells" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells"><img alt="Violet wells" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442260743p2/4196681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/858330333?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Violet wells" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Violet wells" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells">Violet wells</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4196681-violet-wells?shelf=world-war-two">world-war-two</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4196681-violet-wells?shelf=contemporary-british-fiction">contemporary-british-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4196681-violet-wells?shelf=holocaust">holocaust</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer858330333" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8867739108571586563"> In his Afterword Amis pays tribute to a paragraph by Kurt Vonnegut in Slaughterhouse Five where a character watches a backwards-run film of the American planes scooping up bombs from Dresden and miraculously repairing the ruined city, before the bombs are sent back to a factory where all the dangerous contents of their cylinders are separated into harmless minerals. Amis here uses Vonnegut's ingenious tactic of running everything backwards to investigate the holocaust and the men who carried it</span>
  <span id="freeText8867739108571586563" style="display:none"> In his Afterword Amis pays tribute to a paragraph by Kurt Vonnegut in Slaughterhouse Five where a character watches a backwards-run film of the American planes scooping up bombs from Dresden and miraculously repairing the ruined city, before the bombs are sent back to a factory where all the dangerous contents of their cylinders are separated into harmless minerals. Amis here uses Vonnegut's ingenious tactic of running everything backwards to investigate the holocaust and the men who carried it out. <br /><br />You might say Amis's narrator suffers from two conditions which regularly afflict casualties of war and perpetrators of unspeakable acts - dissociative amnesia and split personality disorder. The novel begins with an ageing doctor in New York stumbling backwards from a heart attack. The doctor is the host of our bewildered narrator who discovering no inner life in the doctor only has his dreams to provide clues for what's in store for him. <br /><br />The backwards drift of the narrative, ingeniously sustained, provides lots of fabulous comedy. Churchgoers pocketing money from the collection box; garbage crews strewing rubbish all over the city's pristine streets; pigeons spitting out crumbs for a forsaken individual who takes them home and reconstitutes them into slices of bread. It's a novel that keeps your mind very active in attempts to re-evaluate so many casual things we do every day. Sexual relationships seen backwards also provide some laughs together with the odd disarming insight.<br /><br />I would have liked to have read this not knowing we're eventually going to find ourselves in Auschwitz (the publishers chose clumsily to give away this twist in the blurb no doubt for commercial reasons.) Of course, we now know our doctor is going to heal the Jews and reunite them with their families. It sometimes makes for an uncomfortable reading experience being made to laugh at what happened at Auschwitz but what it does do very powerfully is evoke the idealistic insanity greasing the wheels of the chilling efficiency of the Nazi killing machine. <br /><br />Certainly one thing it does is dump a pie in the face of every loony holocaust denier. <br /><br />I recently read The Sense of an Ending which, broadly speaking, was about remorse. Remorse, one might say, is a dead end. The end of the line. The chilling grey day after Judgement day. Martin Amis here shows us the lengths the human brain will go to avoid remorse. <br /><br />4+ stars.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8867739108571586563" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_840163793" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/840163793" />
    <a title="Darwin8u" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u"><img alt="Darwin8u" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547440402p2/2294090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/840163793?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darwin8u" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darwin8u" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u">Darwin8u</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer840163793" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18243771499294850544"><i>“They're always looking forward to going places they're just coming back from, or regretting doing things they haven't yet done. They say hello when they mean goodbye.”</i> <br/>― Martin Amis, Time's Arrow<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1459313931i/18594186._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='225' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I liked the prose and liked the execution, but there was still something a bit off. A tooth is missing in time's reverse cog making this Amis story rock rather than roll in reverse. I enjoyed the narrative told backward; extracting the real meaning while reading the meaning back to front is a funky brai</span>
  <span id="freeText18243771499294850544" style="display:none"><i>“They're always looking forward to going places they're just coming back from, or regretting doing things they haven't yet done. They say hello when they mean goodbye.”</i> <br>― Martin Amis, Time's Arrow<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1459313931i/18594186._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="225" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I liked the prose and liked the execution, but there was still something a bit off. A tooth is missing in time's reverse cog making this Amis story rock rather than roll in reverse. I enjoyed the narrative told backward; extracting the real meaning while reading the meaning back to front is a funky brain trick. I loved having a Nazi doctor at the center of the story. The movement from physical and moral corruption to a form of innocence uncovered a bit more of the lizard brain for me.<br><br>The problem, however, is bending this story without a need for infinite folds in time. There is no gliding back with prose. There are only jumps back with glides forward. Amis is forced to skip back in time, translate, and then relate the narrative forward. Again and again and again. It was a bit like walking the dog with a yoyo. You are pulling the story one direction, but the narrative SAH|HAS to keep spinning in a reverse direction. The skips are necessary, but still disruptive to the narrative. Anyway, I liked it. It was a good thought exercise, just not great literature. A minor experiment from a very good contemporary writer.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18243771499294850544" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_249870969" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/249870969" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/419287-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289534648p2/419287.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/249870969?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/419287-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">backwards-minded types</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=here-is-new-york">here-is-new-york</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=crime-and-punishment">crime-and-punishment</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=kind-of-depressing">kind-of-depressing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer249870969" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5998161779742036308">She can't help it if her best isn't very good, but she's done it. She's ploddingly typed out her half-assedly apropos review, then clicked on the stars -- three of them, yellow and cartoony, her blithe summation of an author's painstakingly wrought offering to twentieth-century literature. He'll probably spend years writing then researching this thing, which she's already rated like it's an eBay-seller transaction, and reviewed with all the thoughtfulness and care of an Adderall-snorting thirtee</span>
  <span id="freeText5998161779742036308" style="display:none">She can't help it if her best isn't very good, but she's done it. She's ploddingly typed out her half-assedly apropos review, then clicked on the stars -- three of them, yellow and cartoony, her blithe summation of an author's painstakingly wrought offering to twentieth-century literature. He'll probably spend years writing then researching this thing, which she's already rated like it's an eBay-seller transaction, and reviewed with all the thoughtfulness and care of an Adderall-snorting thirteen-year-old's Facebook status update...<br /><br />In any case, now she'll see what this book's all about. She picks it up, name-scans the Afterword (Aw, Hitch!), and begins. Seems to be a fairly standard-sort bildungsroman kind of thing, young boy into man... oh, no, but wait. It's not really -- some heavy stuff here -- and -- uh oh, what's this? -- an arguably silly postmodern TRICK! She likes it well enough, reads the whole thing through in about a day. This author does seem to have got a certain way with words, some nice little descriptive details: "Mickey Mouse sniggers and Greta Garbo averts her pained gaze from [a young couple's] mortified writhings on the shallow fur of cinema seats" (p. 154). Shallow fur! She likes that... Also some nice, darkly-brooding well-phrased stuff with its own intense, seductive style: "There's probably a straightforward explanation for the impossible weariness I feel. A perfectly straightforward explanation. It is a mortal weariness. Maybe I'm tired of being human, if human is what I am. I'm tired of being human" (p. 93). Ooh, that's nice!<br /><br />More good stuff -- time passes from one era to the next with description that transcends mere gimmick... because gimmick is what this is, she sees, as she nears the first page. In this book, she discovers, time runs in reverse, and the life of the main character is being chronicled from the end backwards by a rather hapless, baffled narrator whom we're encouraged to picture as "a sentimentalized fetus, with faithful smile" (p. 42).<br /><br />Does it work? It works. She more or less does get pretty into the whole thing. But then, she's prone to jokes that go on way too long, and tends to find them more amusing in the endless retelling: an old man wearing bellbottoms in the early eighties is fashion's cutting edge, garbage men scatter trash throughout cities, while highway workers rip up the road. Of course, she knows, this is Literature, so sometimes the joke is very Serious: its protagonist is a doctor, who appalls his Jiminy Cricket-type observing ego by brutalizing patients, as doctors in this backwards world (almost) always necessarily do. The narrator speculates on the demolition of cities, centuries from now, into "the pleasant land -- green, promised," and pauses to assert that he's glad he wasn't around for the city's creation. It's poignant, while also cool, as she finds this novel has generally been throughout.<br /><br />By the time she's done, she's resolved to seek out more of the writer's work. Although this isn't the greatest book she's ever read, she enjoyed it, and she bets he's done better elsewhere with this evident cleverness and his linguistic gifts. She adds <i>Time's Arrow</i> to her to-read list, and reviews another book by Amis -- book reviews, <i>The War Against Cliche</i> -- which, when she reads it, she feels is vastly superior. Then she goes on to sample more of his fiction, and finds it sort of beguilingly uneven. As with <i>Time's Arrow</i>, each book has, to varying degrees, both its awe-inspiring strengths and unforgivable flaws. None of what she reads is, in her opinion, as good as his reviews... until she finally comes across <i>London Fields</i>, and is lovestruck: THIS is the Martin Amis novel she's been waiting for all her life!<br /><br />And why is the thing you're looking for always in the last place you look? It seems like she would read more Amis after loving <i>London Fields</i> so much, but she doesn't, and acts surprised later when a close female friend recommends his work. In fact, she seems to forget any real sense of who Amis is, and is overheard sharing a vague negative impression -- acquired who knows where -- that "only pretentious, asshole guys who are way too into coke and themselves read him."<br /><br />Which is too bad, because Martin Amis is a really good writer, and he's written a lot of books, and she might really enjoy some if she gave them a chance. But it's too late. Wait, <i>is</i> it too late? It might be too late, or, alternatively, it might not be... To be honest, she's not sure how this whole thing works, and trying to figure out the logistics sort of makes her head hurt.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5998161779742036308" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_207770668" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/207770668" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/207770668?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099455356" href="/book/show/829035.Time_s_Arrow">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=miscellaneous-fiction">miscellaneous-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=overrated">overrated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer207770668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13970536682190974958"><b>The non-U USP</b><br/>A short book that is one long gimmick: clever as a writing exercise, but not worth publishing or reading. Once the novelty of a backwards story has worn off, there is little point to it and I lost interest (though I did finish it). And it's not even that original: Kurt Vonnegut had the same idea as a brief scene in "Slaughterhouse Five" (see my review <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129649718' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a>) as did Borges in the short story A Weary Man’s Utopia, which is in "The Book of Sand" (see my review <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1322888793' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a>), and probably Fitzge</span>
  <span id="freeText13970536682190974958" style="display:none"><b>The non-U USP</b><br />A short book that is one long gimmick: clever as a writing exercise, but not worth publishing or reading. Once the novelty of a backwards story has worn off, there is little point to it and I lost interest (though I did finish it). And it's not even that original: Kurt Vonnegut had the same idea as a brief scene in "Slaughterhouse Five" (see my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129649718" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>) as did Borges in the short story A Weary Man’s Utopia, which is in "The Book of Sand" (see my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1322888793" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>), and probably Fitzgerald's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/747746.The_Curious_Case_of_Benjamin_Button" title="The Curious Case of Benjamin Button by F. Scott Fitzgerald" rel="nofollow noopener">The Curious Case of Benjamin Button</a>.<br /><br />It opens with painfully vivid descriptions of a life-and death emergency. It turns out to be the story of one man's life, told backwards by a consciousness/conscience inhabiting his body, but with no memory of what is to come (i.e. what has already happened). It feels his emotions, but can't control them or his actions. He is a doctor, so in this world, he assaults people, "money... all comes down to the quality of your trash" and "all sustenance, all meaning" comes from the loo!<br /><br /><b>Sense or Nonsense?</b><br />At one point the narrator says: <br />"<i>I have noticed... that most conversations would make much better sense if you ran them backwards. But with this man-woman stuff, you could run them any way you liked - and still get no further forward.</i>" <br />There are duly several scenes where it is quite intriguing to read the dialog forwards then backwards, and the fact it works is clever, but... so what?<br /><br /><b>Page or Screen?</b><br />I can't work out if it would be better or worse as a film: backwards footage of walking and eating is passé and comedic, but some other things would work well:<br />"<i>The ship's route is clearly delineated on the surface of the water and is violently consumed by our advance. Thus we leave no mark on the ocean, as if we were covering our tracks.</i>"<br /><br /><b>Gratuituous Gimmicks?</b><br />There are some other ideas where running them backwards gives an intriguing or awkwardly funny slant, but they don't add up to a decent novel, and some of them are so gratuitous and irrelevant to the plot (e.g. buying teeth from the tooth fairy) that I can't help thinking Amis had a list of backward things he wanted to incorporate. <br /><br />The slightly more interesting ones include the "meticulous vandalism" of gardening and "uglify the home" instead of DIY, birth being a long, painful goodbye, "a wounded finger healed and sealed by the knife's blade", hippies going to Vietnam and returning sane, middle age resurgence of interest in sex being like puberty, breaking up reading like a slushy reconciliation, and bottling the gook from one's hair and selling it. But ultimately, they're a series of gimmicks.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13970536682190974958" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2398224696" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2398224696?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=postmodern-fiction">postmodern-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=great-britain">great-britain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2398224696" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1672957498507228031">It's not often I go into a novel thinking there's a high chance that I'm going to absolutely hate it, only to be completely surprised by just how much I liked it. Well, that's the case with Time's Arrow. <br/>I tend to find, although not always, problems with Holocaust fiction, tending to stick to non-fiction, and part of me thought this would turn out to be nothing more than a piece of artsy nonsense. A self-indulgent literary gimmick. And about such a serious topic as well. But it worked for me.<br/><br/>The</span>
  <span id="freeText1672957498507228031" style="display:none">It's not often I go into a novel thinking there's a high chance that I'm going to absolutely hate it, only to be completely surprised by just how much I liked it. Well, that's the case with Time's Arrow. <br />I tend to find, although not always, problems with Holocaust fiction, tending to stick to non-fiction, and part of me thought this would turn out to be nothing more than a piece of artsy nonsense. A self-indulgent literary gimmick. And about such a serious topic as well. But it worked for me.<br /><br />The central character is a former Nazi medical experimenter under Mengele at Auschwitz, now known as Tod Friendly, a.k.a John Young, a.k.a Odilo Unverdorben, who is hiding out in the suburbs as the novel opens. But is 'opens' quite the right word to use? As what we have here is a cleverish narrative where the clock Runs Backwards in time. We start at the end of Tod’s existence, and finish up with the commencement, as he is about to be reborn.<br />It’s quite an intense experience, reading of someone getting younger rather than older, and even though the concept may not be easy to get grip on intellectually, it works like a charm intuitively and on an emotional level. Amis' playfulness and teasing quality actually heightens the impact on the reader. It takes historical events that have been recited often, and brings us right up against them suddenly from new and unexpected angles, but angles that still retain the sombre power to shock us all over again. Any dialogue used is equally in reverse order, so instead of reading a conversation backwards, the trick is to read up from the bottom for them to take full effect. <br />Working as a doctor in the early chapters, Tod seems to make people worse rather than better.<br />They come into his office feeling great, and stagger out looking pretty awful. As Herr Doctor Odilo Unverdorben of Auschwitz on the other hand, Tod comes on like a miracle healer, and not just of individuals. All that horrendous destruction is undone right before our eyes, as countless shattered lives - children, families, cultures, and entire nations, are magically reconstituted in all their original splendor. <br /><br />There’s no denying that Time’s Arrow is playful in nature. And on the surface a fair portion of its entertainment value derives from the zest with which Amis works out the implications of his premise. Because Amis delivers the result first and the cause afterwards, he can toy around with us, peppering the narrative with incidental puzzles. On a deeper level though, it can still be seen as something more threatening.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1672957498507228031" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1305275616" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14500903046169811110">We can never change our past. <br/><br/>No matter how bad we were, no matter how good we were -- time, the man-made structure that decides what we do in our lives, how often we celebrate occasions, when we are born, when we die, what people think about us after we die, does not discriminate.<br/><br/>The concept behind Amis's 'Time's Arrow' is gimmicky at its core, but works only by the intelligence and craftiness of its author.<br/><br/>When reading this, you see the outlook from a man with a possible multiple personality s</span>
  <span id="freeText14500903046169811110" style="display:none">We can never change our past. <br /><br />No matter how bad we were, no matter how good we were -- time, the man-made structure that decides what we do in our lives, how often we celebrate occasions, when we are born, when we die, what people think about us after we die, does not discriminate.<br /><br />The concept behind Amis's 'Time's Arrow' is gimmicky at its core, but works only by the intelligence and craftiness of its author.<br /><br />When reading this, you see the outlook from a man with a possible multiple personality speaking from its point of view in reverse chronology and not the central character, a man who has escaped the ills of his past to change his life, hide from the past, needing the chance to heal again. <br /><br />This work of art takes patience and focus. We, as a whole, as everything, move forward and only see one perspective. Reading it from a reverse point of view seems comical at times, but also horrendous when the natural act was anything but the sort. <br /><br />Some examples:<br /><br /><i>
  <b>"They're always looking forward to going places they're just coming back from, or regretting doing things they haven't yet done. They say hello when they mean goodbye."</b>
</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>"It’s all strange to me. I know I live on a fierce and magical planet, which sheds or surrenders rain or even flings it off in whipstroke after whipstroke, which fires out bolts of electric gold into the firmament at 186,000 miles per second, which with a single shrug of its tectonic plates can erect a city in half an hour. Creation … is easy, is quick. There’s also a universe, apparently. But I cannot bear to see the stars, even though I know they’re there all right, and I do see them, because Tod looks upward at night, as everybody does, and coos and points. The Plough. Sirius, the dog. The stars, to me, are like pins and needles, are like the routemap of a nightmare. Don’t join the dots.… Of the stars, one alone can I contemplate without pain. And that’s a planet. The planet they call the evening star, the morning star. Intense Venus."</b>
</i><br /><br />Many may get tired of it within the first few pages, but stick with it and you'll be rewarded. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14500903046169811110" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_133284320" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/133284320" />
    <a title="Jen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1719442-jen"><img alt="Jen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451874923p2/1719442.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/133284320?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jen" href="/user/show/1719442-jen">Jen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780517585153" href="/book/show/534339.Time_s_Arrow_or_The_Nature_of_the_Offense">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1719442-jen?shelf=silver-shelf">silver-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1719442-jen?shelf=exacto-knives-are-nice-and-shiny">exacto-knives-are-nice-and-shiny</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1719442-jen?shelf=lit-fic">lit-fic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133284320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12559851063946647932"><i>English Standard Version (©2001)<br/>For now we see in a mirror dimly, but then face to face. Now I know in part; then I shall know fully, even as I have been fully known.</i><br/><br/><i>“What is it with them, the human beings? I suppose they remember what they want to remember.”<br/>-Time’s Arrow</i><br/><br/> This is what I want to remember: that I bought this off a wheeled cart for two quarters. That in a bad economy, this was a great investment. Amis is genius in this book. Pure genius. His structure starts with the last rattling </span>
  <span id="freeText12559851063946647932" style="display:none"><i>English Standard Version (©2001)<br />For now we see in a mirror dimly, but then face to face. Now I know in part; then I shall know fully, even as I have been fully known.</i><br /><br /><i>“What is it with them, the human beings? I suppose they remember what they want to remember.”<br />-Time’s Arrow</i><br /><br /> This is what I want to remember: that I bought this off a wheeled cart for two quarters. That in a bad economy, this was a great investment. Amis is genius in this book. Pure genius. His structure starts with the last rattling gasp of life and then pulls the reader backward, reanimating a character’s troubled past by making it the future of the narrative. <br /> I knew going into this book that it was about the holocaust. What I did not know was that the holocaust could still move me, shock me, and brutalize me anymore. I had read too many books on it, seen too many movies and documentaries about it. I became stupid and calloused and let this lull me into thinking that the holocaust had been overdone. Overdone (!) as in boilerplate, as in Hollywood and botox. Thankfully, this book shamed me back into grief. <br /> Reading this book and trying to get a grasp on the main character is a little like trying to figure out what your face really looks like by only using the side of a spoon for a mirror. The narrator is hard to figure out; it may be the broken and detached voice of the main character’s conscience or it may not. Either way, the narrator is confused. Everything is off. Reality is blurred and warped. Things are born from fire instead of read and then burned, tumors are strategically buried inside bodies and not removed, color televisions are traded in for black and white ones. The all-seeing eye that moves with Doctor Tod (“Death”) Friendly is on perpetual rewind and rarely sees things clearly. <i>“But wait a minute. The baby is crawling, only one or two panting inches at a time- but crawling forward. Hey! Christ, how long has it been since I…? Anyhow, it’s soon over, this lucid interval. The mother is reading backward again, and the baby is merely weeping. It wants its diaper filled with new shit from the trash. I’m being immature. I’ve got to get over it. I keep expecting the world to make sense. It doesn’t. It won’t. Ever.”</i> By using this approach, Amis is able to make sudden and profound statements on life, man, and society, and he is able to keep the time period and setting from completely overwhelming the story. <br /> Most readers know to expect violence in a story involving the Holocaust, and expectations are fulfilled here in the most striking manner. What is surprising about Time’s Arrow is where the violence fails to occur. Going counterclockwise in this story means reading passages where upholding the Hippocratic Oath is detestable and where injecting victims with acid or delivering Zyklon B is portrayed as almost a heartwarming gesture. <i> “We’d just totaled a couple of teenage boys. Their mothers has brought them in and then got the hell out soon after we’d started work, staying only to witness the methodical unraveling of the soaked bandages. We took the stitches out and swabbed the boys with blood. I remember Witney’s skillful insertion of some kind of crossbow bolt; me, I was wedging shards of brown glass into the other boys’ crown. And we both, as they say, ‘cracked up.’ We laughed at each other, full face, showing at last with teeth and tongue and tonsils the mortal hilarity that sniggers behind everything we do here. Our laughter, together with the boys’ cries and whimpers…”</i><br />Instead of breaking up families coming off the trains at Treblinka, the doctor plays matchmaker. How kind the good Nazi doctor is when he takes his own gold and fills the Jews’ teeth. The author creates a revisionist history here, winking at the reader, while the reader nods, smiles, and takes away a fuller knowledge of the truth. <br /> The whole thing is amazing, really. It takes a master craftsman to construct a place where <i>“time’s arrow moves the other way.”</i> The phrase “Life is best understood backwards” takes on depth here, and the author is thorough, linking the book’s title with the clocks at Treblinka, there <i>“…to reassure the Jews- the Jews of Warsaw, Radom, and the Bialystok districts whom the camp had serviced…every station, every journey, needs a clock. The hands were painted and would never move to an earlier time. Beneath the clock was an enormous arrow, on which was printed: ‘Change Here For Eastern Trains.’ But time had no arrow, not here.” </i> Brutal, the forethought of that painted clock. And there, right there, the author makes a winning case for the suspension of time’s correct movement throughout his work. Time is gone here, in this book, because time was suspended and removed during that ugly period in history as well. <br />This book is dark. Hope does not spring eternal here, but instead gives into the dark side, staring into the bleak void of a starved and hollowed eyed nihilism. It is disturbing, really, to read Amis’ words from a narrator that is like<i> “a baby taken from the toilet,”</i> having a heart but no face: <i>“We cry and twist and are naked at both ends of life. We cry at both ends of life while the doctor watches.”</i> If human beings remember only what they want to, I want to remember this book, if only to keep me from forgetting to tremble at the horrors of history.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12559851063946647932" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/156503674" />
    <a title="WILLIAM2" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4684009-william2"><img alt="WILLIAM2" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530030828p2/4684009.jpg" /></a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer156503674" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4506361085385763995">Second reading. Just brilliant. See my review of Kurt Vonnegut's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4981.Slaughterhouse_Five' title='Slaughterhouse-Five by Kurt Vonnegut' rel='nofollow noopener'>Slaughterhouse-Five</a> for my theory that <i>Time's Arrow</i> was inspired, at least in part, by a sequence in that earlier novel in which the protagonist watches a war film running backwards. Highly recommended.</span>
  <span id="freeText4506361085385763995" style="display:none">Second reading. Just brilliant. <div id="review_139117603" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/139117603" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4702742-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1348673712p2/4702742.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/139117603?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/4702742-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer139117603" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1433560110232906611">After reading the first page of this book and realising that Amis was actually going to write a novel with time moving backwards I thought he must have some brilliant notion that required and would more than excuse the use of such an gimmicky device. I was willing to overlook all the technical and conceptual failings and inconsistencies in execution, on grounds of artistic licence, with the faith that the payoff would be so clever, insightful and illuminating theses trivial concerns would pale i</span>
  <span id="freeText1433560110232906611" style="display:none">After reading the first page of this book and realising that Amis was actually going to write a novel with time moving backwards I thought he must have some brilliant notion that required and would more than excuse the use of such an gimmicky device. I was willing to overlook all the technical and conceptual failings and inconsistencies in execution, on grounds of artistic licence, with the faith that the payoff would be so clever, insightful and illuminating theses trivial concerns would pale into insignificance. In-fact I thought that there were so many failings and inconsistencies was proof of the confidence Amis had in the magnitude of the payoff that was to come. <br /> <br />I found myself at what was clearly meant to be the apex of the novel thinking “is this it?” and wondering what it was that I was supposed to be taking from the notion of a concentration camp running backwards in time. All the technical flaws aside, what was it that Amis was hoping to say with this book? I really have no idea. The holocaust has many disturbing and uncomfortable lessons to teach us about human nature. Many that are so close to the bone that over 60 years on and after significant engagement with the subject we have barely scratched the surface of the harrowing idea that the capacity for such evil lies within any of us.<br /> <br />Needless to say this is extremely fertile and important ground for artists. In-fact it is one of the areas in which humanity truly has to look to it artists for guidance and understanding. And while I respect any artistic attempt to engage with this subject matter I feel Amis has really missed the mark with this book. So much so I’m not even really sure what he was aiming at in the first place.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1433560110232906611" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/265615800" />
    <a title="David Lentz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3604115-david-lentz"><img alt="David Lentz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473090881p2/3604115.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/265615800?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Lentz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Lentz" href="/user/show/3604115-david-lentz">David Lentz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer265615800" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6175866215462527706">"Time's Arrow" is a very fine and powerful novel by Amis who, for me, had a tough encore after reading his genius novel, "Money," of which stratospheric literary level "Time's Arrow" falls a smidge short. However, "Time's Arrow" is very well conceived, highly inventive, lyrically narrated and powerful in its dire themes ultimately relating to one man's poignant personal relationship to the Holocaust. Amis deploys with great skill the narrative device of telling one man's story backward in a disc</span>
  <span id="freeText6175866215462527706" style="display:none">"Time's Arrow" is a very fine and powerful novel by Amis who, for me, had a tough encore after reading his genius novel, "Money," of which stratospheric literary level "Time's Arrow" falls a smidge short. However, "Time's Arrow" is very well conceived, highly inventive, lyrically narrated and powerful in its dire themes ultimately relating to one man's poignant personal relationship to the Holocaust. Amis deploys with great skill the narrative device of telling one man's story backward in a disciplined linear story arc from death to birth. We start at a point in the narrator's current life and systematically point the arrow of time in reverse so that each line of narrative, including dialogue, goes straight into flight to an immediately prior point of time until the narrator is three years old. Amis shows an adept skill for showing how, when the narrative takes the reader from the present in a linear flight, the results seem surprising and contradictory. At first this narrative technique seems confusing and even disorienting until the reader understands the narrative vocabulary of the retreat and the rich irony of looking backward event by event. There are also dualing personal voices in the single narrator which require some imagination to sort out but which lend intrigue to the narrative device of time's reversal and that reversal's impact upon the assessment of fortune. Amis genuinely is a major literary novelist and his writing is reliably spectacular as he crafts lines which are pithy, ironic, tragicomic and compelling. I truly respect the writing in "Time's Arrow" and Martin Amis whose masterpiece, "Money," I recommend that you read first.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6175866215462527706" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_13009593" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13009593" />
    <a title="Beverly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/804601-beverly"><img alt="Beverly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1200808957p2/804601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13009593?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beverly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beverly" href="/user/show/804601-beverly">Beverly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who don't care for Martin Amis's other books</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer13009593" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15228778774416584140">It continues to amaze me how those who claim to be fans of Martin Amis haven't heard of or read Time's Arrow. This book is a masterpiece in experimental fiction. He literally, methodically, writes the story backwards as his character experiences time going backwards. I don't know of any other author who has attempted and succeeded in doing this. It's been a while since I read it, but what I remember was the uncanny sense that I was experiencing time backwards as I read it. I began questioning wh</span>
  <span id="freeText15228778774416584140" style="display:none">It continues to amaze me how those who claim to be fans of Martin Amis haven't heard of or read Time's Arrow. This book is a masterpiece in experimental fiction. He literally, methodically, writes the story backwards as his character experiences time going backwards. I don't know of any other author who has attempted and succeeded in doing this. It's been a while since I read it, but what I remember was the uncanny sense that I was experiencing time backwards as I read it. I began questioning what was happening to the event of reading, to me!, through this process of a narrative that runs itself backwards. It was requiring that I begin to read the narrative differently, facts collected themselves on the pages chronologically backwards, cause and effect were reversed, action and responses were twisted, requiring the reader to think differently, to read differently. It's quite fascinating, and quite an amazing accomplishment for a writer. I am looking forward to finding the time to read it again soon. (note: I have tried his other books and never could get interested in them like I did this one. Tells me that if you don't usually like Martin Amis's books, you would probably like this one.)<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15228778774416584140" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2993519186" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2993519186" />
    <a title="Emily May" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may"><img alt="Emily May" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528390751p2/4622890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2993519186?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily May" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may">Emily May</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780517585153" href="/book/show/534339.Time_s_Arrow_or_The_Nature_of_the_Offense">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=2019">2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2993519186" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13446800659528819502"><blockquote>
  <i>When people move-- when they travel --they look where they've come from, not where they're going. Is this what the human beings always do?</i>
</blockquote><br/>I have apparently read Martin Amis before. My <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/733586817' rel='nofollow noopener'>goodreads review</a> tells me I read his <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18825.Money' title='Money by Martin Amis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Money</a> in 2016, but I had to read through my comments again to remember a single thing about it. I'm giving <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23031.Time_s_Arrow' title='Time's Arrow by Martin Amis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Time's Arrow</a> a similarly middling rating, though I <i>think</i> this one will stay with me longer, if for no other reason than it required a decent amount of thought and effor</span>
  <span id="freeText13446800659528819502" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>When people move-- when they travel --they look where they've come from, not where they're going. Is this what the human beings always do?</i>
</blockquote><br />I have apparently read Martin Amis before. My <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/733586817" rel="nofollow noopener">goodreads review</a> tells me I read his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18825.Money" title="Money by Martin Amis" rel="nofollow noopener">Money</a> in 2016, but I had to read through my comments again to remember a single thing about it. I'm giving <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23031.Time_s_Arrow" title="Time's Arrow by Martin Amis" rel="nofollow noopener">Time's Arrow</a> a similarly middling rating, though I <i>think</i> this one will stay with me longer, if for no other reason than it required a decent amount of thought and effort on my part.<br /><br />The premise is interesting, if a bit gimmicky. We follow Tod T. Friendly as he comes back from death, grows younger and fitter day by day, changes his name, moves across the world and returns to his youth as a doctor in Auschwitz. All of this is narrated by a presence, a doppelganger inside of Tod who is separate from Tod's thoughts and consciousness. I got sold pretty quickly on the idea of telling a man's life story backwards, but I was never fully sold on this separate consciousness. I don't understand why a regular third-person narrative wouldn't have sufficed; the only thing missing would be the narrator's constant exclamations about how nothing makes sense (obviously, because it's happening in reverse).<br /><br />It took me some time to get used to the narrative going backwards. Even though I knew what was going on, my mind really wanted to put things in the order I was reading them. It required a number of stops to think about the actual forward chronology of events (like of course he wasn't stealing from the church collection tray, he was putting money in it). It was especially difficult with conversations where we would get the answers before we knew what the questions were. I had to read each one backwards after to fully understand it.<br /><br />It also doesn't work perfectly. Though conversations happen backwards, the individual words and sentences do not. If one were really to experience life in reverse, they would hear a series of gobbledygook words, but obviously Amis couldn't be <i>that</i> cruel to us. And while he avoids most talk of eating, on the occasions where it occurs, the characters are described as eating their food, not regurgitating it onto their plates. Honestly, the downside of having to think about everything you read means you also notice every little inconsistency too.<br /><br />However, I do think it was quite interesting to look at a man's life in reverse. Reading about a man getting younger and knowing less, becoming more innocent and naive, and falling in love while we already know how it ends... there is a certain sadness to it. To conclude the book not with an old man who has committed horrors, but with a helpless infant who is yet to make those mistakes, is definitely effective.<br /><br /><a href="http://emilymay.me" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.facebook.com/thebookgeekreviews" rel="nofollow noopener">Facebook</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/thebookgeek01" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="http://instagram.com/emleemay" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://www.youtube.com/c/EmilyMayTheBookGeek" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13446800659528819502" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/167344583" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/192703-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581612438p2/192703.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/167344583?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/192703-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099455356" href="/book/show/829035.Time_s_Arrow">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer167344583" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14897027904352552483">Normally when I sense that a writer is going to pull a stunt with the entire conceit of his or her novel, I end up with a slow disdainful Billy Idol-style grimace developing on my face before thudding against the glass ceiling of disgust and shutting the book for good.<br/><br/>Don't do it, Martin. You don't have to dazzle us with a technical feat like this. You're too good for that. And it's called "trying too hard..."<br/><br/>Still, Martin must've been kicking around novel ideas when, probably a little buzzed, g</span>
  <span id="freeText14897027904352552483" style="display:none">Normally when I sense that a writer is going to pull a stunt with the entire conceit of his or her novel, I end up with a slow disdainful Billy Idol-style grimace developing on my face before thudding against the glass ceiling of disgust and shutting the book for good.<br /><br />Don't do it, Martin. You don't have to dazzle us with a technical feat like this. You're too good for that. And it's called "trying too hard..."<br /><br />Still, Martin must've been kicking around novel ideas when, probably a little buzzed, gazing at the sunset during a vacation in Portugal or Maiorca, he must've thought: "Hey, I can write anything I want... Isn't it my DUTY to push the limits of fiction? I mean, I could even try to write a freaking book about a Nazi war criminal BACKWARDS!!!" <br /><br />Sure, go for it, Martin. Then burn the manuscript after twenty pages because it's going to suck, it's gonna be just you showing off your crafty technical brilliance and we're all going to end up feeling seasick, singing White Wedding.<br /><br />But he went for it (there had to be many moments when he thought "this isn't working") and sure enough, not only does he dazzle with the brilliance of the technical high-wire act, he makes damn thing work.<br /><br />Amis makes the time-moving-backward conceit neccessary. You understand the development of the character in a way that would be, obviously, impossible by any other means. The character opens up in reverse. <br /><br />Not only does it work, it ends up breaking your fucking heart.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14897027904352552483" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1309441" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/88295-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1232290261p2/88295.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1309441?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/88295-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780517585153" href="/book/show/534339.Time_s_Arrow_or_The_Nature_of_the_Offense">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1309441" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9605670423461137259">I can't say enough about this novel, though a quick glance at my friends' reviews reveals that they liked it but were not quite as blown away by it. I loved how Amis took a conceit (running the world backwards and witnessing it from a naive viewpoint that must make sense of backwards-living) and used it to make new something that had grown shopworn and overfamiliar: Literature about the Holocaust. The novel is howlingly funny, and just when you want it to gain in seriousness and gravity, it does</span>
  <span id="freeText9605670423461137259" style="display:none">I can't say enough about this novel, though a quick glance at my friends' reviews reveals that they liked it but were not quite as blown away by it. I loved how Amis took a conceit (running the world backwards and witnessing it from a naive viewpoint that must make sense of backwards-living) and used it to make new something that had grown shopworn and overfamiliar: Literature about the Holocaust. The novel is howlingly funny, and just when you want it to gain in seriousness and gravity, it does--the book deepens and becomes about the human condition, and about the nature of the soul and sin. In fact, between the galleys of the book and the final printing, Amis removed two words from the final line: "And I, [the soul] within, who came along too late or too early to make a difference." (I am writing this from memory, so if it is a misquote, my apologies. The book is in a box somewhere.) An awesome novel, in the truest sense of "awesome."</span>
  <a data-text-id="9605670423461137259" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56458845-faye?shelf=twentieth-century-classics">twentieth-century-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56458845-faye?shelf=i-own-a-physical-copy">i-own-a-physical-copy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56458845-faye?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56458845-faye?shelf=best-of-2016">best-of-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56458845-faye?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1802033219" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11851390801384135452">Read: November 2016<br/>Rating: 5/5 stars, best of 2016<br/><br/>The plot: the narrator of this story inhabits the dying body of a man named Tod Friendly, and it soon becomes clear that the narrator is living backwards in time, as Tod becomes younger, loses and gains lovers, and moves from his current country back through Portugal and then to Poland and Auschwitz.<br/><br/>I loved this book. It is so beautifully and cleverly written. It is a wonderful contrast to <i>Counter Clock World</i> by Philip K Dick which deals in a sim</span>
  <span id="freeText11851390801384135452" style="display:none">Read: November 2016<br />Rating: 5/5 stars, best of 2016<br /><br />The plot: the narrator of this story inhabits the dying body of a man named Tod Friendly, and it soon becomes clear that the narrator is living backwards in time, as Tod becomes younger, loses and gains lovers, and moves from his current country back through Portugal and then to Poland and Auschwitz.<br /><br />I loved this book. It is so beautifully and cleverly written. It is a wonderful contrast to <i>Counter Clock World</i> by Philip K Dick which deals in a similar reality. Dick however deals with time moving backwards on a social and global scale; how it affects society and culture, whereas Amis never loses his focus on Tod.<br /><br />What struck me the most about <i>Time's Arrow</i> is that the Narrator (who doesn't appear to *be* Tod) observes a world that is so much better than ours in a way. He sees for example, men removing the fur lining of their coats to clothe helpless animals, he sees Auschwitz as a place where the dead and the dying are returned to life and health. Where the soldiers and prison guards bestow small kindnesses on their charges; stopping women here and there in the camp to give them gifts of rings and bracelets, putting body parts back together on an operating table to bring a teenage boy back to life. It is heartbreaking to realise what the narrator is seeing is actually the opposite of reality.<br /><br />Amis has achieved something incredible for me with this novel. He has shown me a different perspective and a different way of viewing the world we live in. What a thoroughly deserving nominee for the Booker prize.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11851390801384135452" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer440747014" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13375199808866432193">Hmm, everyone's reviews say he wrote the book backwards, when it's untrue; the book is written from beginning to end, like all of them. It just tells the story from the end to the beginning, but in order. <br/>My age-old Amis question remains: why is he so drawn to the dark and nasty? I was reading over my infant son's shoulder while I was feeding him, and it was so icky I felt like I was transferring some kind of violence to him bodily. There's always a - prurient, almost - attraction to violence. W</span>
  <span id="freeText13375199808866432193" style="display:none">Hmm, everyone's reviews say he wrote the book backwards, when it's untrue; the book is written from beginning to end, like all of them. It just tells the story from the end to the beginning, but in order. <br />My age-old Amis question remains: why is he so drawn to the dark and nasty? I was reading over my infant son's shoulder while I was feeding him, and it was so icky I felt like I was transferring some kind of violence to him bodily. There's always a - prurient, almost - attraction to violence. What is with that? I am repelled by it. Like, almost, that he wanted to write this book so he could contstruct violence out of healing (which is what happens when things are bassackwards, see). It's also very smart, he's always a brilliant writer in terms of the prose, and clever, he plays nicely with the concept, but at the same time I kept losing my suspension of disbelief at times like, say, when he would run a conversation in reverse order, but not the individual sentences, or words. Events would be bundled into blocks and the blocks assembled backwards, but still in themselves reading from left to right; a distraction that he created himself with his ambitious damn ambition, I say. Make the bed with a blanket of gimmicks and lie in it.<br />Anyway, it was good, and I would have whipped through it in a couple of days if it wasn't for this new little baby thing. Which is I suspect how it should be read, a short sharp concepty nugget.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13375199808866432193" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_381686949" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/381686949" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/381686949?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jenny (Reading Envy) by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/7663454-dale">Dale</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=ebooks">ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=read2014">read2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=open-book">open-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=sci-fi-fantasy">sci-fi-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer381686949" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6473265952832153050">"An old lady descends from the black branches of the fire escape every morning and wearily gathers it all up and clambers home with it in paper bags: the food left for her by the birds."<br/><br/>Before I say what I think about this novel, I should acknowledge that this idea of traveling backwards in time is not one that comes from Amis. Several people have accused him of stealing it from Vonnegut in Slaughterhouse-Five, in fact Amis himself acknowledges that inspiration in his afterward. A few years befo</span>
  <span id="freeText6473265952832153050" style="display:none">"An old lady descends from the black branches of the fire escape every morning and wearily gathers it all up and clambers home with it in paper bags: the food left for her by the birds."<br /><br />Before I say what I think about this novel, I should acknowledge that this idea of traveling backwards in time is not one that comes from Amis. Several people have accused him of stealing it from Vonnegut in Slaughterhouse-Five, in fact Amis himself acknowledges that inspiration in his afterward. A few years before Vonnegut published that better known novel was a novel (preceded by a short story with the same concept) by Philip K. Dick, Counter-Clock World, where time travels backwards, babies are shoved back into any available womb, and librarians are evil destroyers of knowledge.<br /><br />It's possible that Amis has never read Dick. But it was impossible for me to read this without thinking of the ideas in that novel that I first read with Dick instead of Amis. There are no points for originality here. But if I go along with the assumption that there is nothing new under the sun and grant him a reprieve, it is still an enjoyable book. Each chapter moves farther back in time although the narrator doesn't understand that is what is happening. The reader knows because of details in the surrounding story. The narrator inhabits the body of a doctor, and works backwards in time towards a secret that he doesn't fully grasp and I'd hate to spoil.<br /><br />Because of this framework of backwards motion time, there are some beautiful things happening (and some disgusting, but that's a given)... people created from ashes, women who spread dirt around homes, a government that sends uniformed men out to spread rubbish at every home, taking "the stitches out and swabbing the boys with blood." As the reader, I was baffled by conversations and had to find their ending/starting places to make sense of them. (Thank goodness Amis didn't flip all the words in reverse or we'd be in Hoban territory.) I think this is an enjoyable read, and I'm looking forward to hearing the author speak at the <a href="http://artsandsciences.sc.edu/theopenbook/node/192" rel="nofollow noopener">Open Book series</a> at the University of South Carolina. I wish I knew more about his more recent works, but this was a good place to start.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6473265952832153050" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1225361153" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1225361153" />
    <a title="Craig Stone" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6191606-craig-stone"><img alt="Craig Stone" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1462533456p2/6191606.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1225361153?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Craig Stone" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Craig Stone" href="/user/show/6191606-craig-stone">Craig Stone</a>
