        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1225361153" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15413632071702269694">I thought this was exceptional. A book that you can read in a day, that challenges the brain into thinking backwards. Throw in the holocaust, suffering and a general bleakness coupled with horror and it's a book worthy of your time. Some people have complained that the words didn't effect them emotionally, but the narrator is the consciousness of a Nazi war criminal raised in pre-war Germany - a dead man in a dead time entering a dead zone. Perhaps there is no emotion, but if there was, it would</span>
  <span id="freeText15413632071702269694" style="display:none">I thought this was exceptional. A book that you can read in a day, that challenges the brain into thinking backwards. Throw in the holocaust, suffering and a general bleakness coupled with horror and it's a book worthy of your time. Some people have complained that the words didn't effect them emotionally, but the narrator is the consciousness of a Nazi war criminal raised in pre-war Germany - a dead man in a dead time entering a dead zone. Perhaps there is no emotion, but if there was, it would be a betrayal on the main character.<br /><br />Taking the original approach aside, Amis can write - the book is littered with lovely phrases and thoughts. <br /><br />A short original book that takes you somewhere else entirely and works within its own rules is a difficult skill to master, yet Amis has not only mastered it, he's added his own spin and it works.</span>

<div id="review_983925562" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/983925562" />
    <a title="Stela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2585926-stela"><img alt="Stela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417111590p2/2585926.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/983925562?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stela" href="/user/show/2585926-stela">Stela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140167795" href="/book/show/1188780.Time_s_Arrow">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2585926-stela?shelf=postmodernism">postmodernism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2585926-stela?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer983925562" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11300486167594348119"><blockquote>
  <b>A Future to Remember</b>
</blockquote><br/><br/>“We should be born old/ Come to life wise/ Be able to decide our place in the world/ Know from the first crossroad which way to go…” says Ana Blandiana, a Romanian poet, in a beautiful poem that seems to anticipate the extraordinary novel of Martin Amis, <i>Time’s Arrow</i>.<br/><br/>Indeed, the inverted time seems to be the main theme, developed with a narrative technique that uses the back to back story to answer an intertextual question through a narrator whose role gets new valences. </span>
  <span id="freeText11300486167594348119" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <b>A Future to Remember</b>
</blockquote><br /><br />“We should be born old/ Come to life wise/ Be able to decide our place in the world/ Know from the first crossroad which way to go…” says Ana Blandiana, a Romanian poet, in a beautiful poem that seems to anticipate the extraordinary novel of Martin Amis, <i>Time’s Arrow</i>.<br /><br />Indeed, the inverted time seems to be the main theme, developed with a narrative technique that uses the back to back story to answer an intertextual question through a narrator whose role gets new valences. <br /><br />It is true that Martin Amis’s technique is not entirely new. Inspired by the backwards shooting used in filmmaking, it was used in literature too, although I think this is the first novel to truly turn chronology upside-down <u>all along the story</u>, challenging the reader to also think, and sometimes to read, backwards. For example, there is a deliciously absurd dialogue at pages 34-35, that becomes boringly intelligible while read backwards, that is at pages 35-34. Other times the dialogue can be read either way without making more or less sense. But the real challenge is to remember that not only the conversations, but also the actions and emotions are reversed. The process of eating begins in the garbage and finishes with a clean plate, people grow stronger and younger, cruelties reverse to compassion and hate turns into love. Why? Because in this universe and only in it,<br /><br /><blockquote>Destruction – is difficult. Destruction is slow. Creation, as I said, is no trouble at all.</blockquote> <br /><br />It would seem that this is the answer to redeem the world, by visiting the past to repair it. And for Tod Friendly, aka John Young, aka Hamilton de Souza aka Odilo Unverdorben, the Nazi doctor, there is a lot of repairing to do as he begins his journey from death to Auschwitz and Treblinka, not only to undo what he has done but also to better understand the nature of the offence Primo Levi spoke about, that offence that robbed both victims and perpetrators of their humanity. Backwards and backwards he goes, from the nightmares to their source, from the fears to their cause, from the “what” to “why”. There, in Anus Mundi, for “The Auschwitz universe, it has to be allowed, was fiercely coprocentric”, the narrator becomes aware that, for the first time, the world is intelligible:<br /><br /><blockquote>The world, after all, here in Auschwitz, has a new habit. It makes sense.</blockquote> <br /><br />The world <u>and</u> the journey. Whose sense has nothing to do with the character who keeps forgetting the future while regressing in time (for if humanity has learnt nothing from the past how could it learn from the future?), but with the narrator, maybe the most interesting presence in the novel, fulfilling several roles: of a witness, of the main character’s double, of the creator.<br /><br />As a witness and a double he followed Odilo in the history pit to dig up, from the trash and the shit, the nature of the offence, which was the abnormal marriage between civilization and savagery, between intellect and abjection:<br /><br /><blockquote>No longer can I bear with the ruined god, betrayed and beaten by his own magic. Calling on powers best left unsummoned, he took human beings apart – and then he put them back together again.</blockquote> <br /><br />The second journey he took followed the creation process, in which the time has no arrow since it is the creator’s task to make us remember the errors of the past and future, to make us question our humanity, like Primo Levi did. <br /><br />And, also like Levi and all the other great artists, to remind us that art is our only redemption.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11300486167594348119" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3188542836" />
    <a title="Nigel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61463964-nigel"><img alt="Nigel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513313791p2/61463964.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3188542836?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer1257090475447615362">This novel was very difficult to classify - if you don't want spoilers, don't read the synopsis/blurb, or any of the recommendations. I made the mistake of doing this when I was about halfway through the book, and it ruined the build up and ending for me, as it gives it all away.<br/>Clever, witty, sophisticated. <br/>The narrator of this book is the protagonist's soul, or conscience. The kicker is that the conscience (who is unable to influence the characters behaviour or thoughts, so maybe it is not his</span>
  <span id="freeText1257090475447615362" style="display:none">This novel was very difficult to classify - if you don't want spoilers, don't read the synopsis/blurb, or any of the recommendations. I made the mistake of doing this when I was about halfway through the book, and it ruined the build up and ending for me, as it gives it all away.<br />Clever, witty, sophisticated. <br />The narrator of this book is the protagonist's soul, or conscience. The kicker is that the conscience (who is unable to influence the characters behaviour or thoughts, so maybe it is not his conscience, but maybe his soul), comes into being/sentience at the end of the character's life, then progresses through life backwards. So the novel tells the story of a life backwards. I won't tell you what that life entails, as this gives it away, but it is a novel of the twentieth century.<br />The comic elements come from this device, with clever descriptions of how this is experienced in relation to romantic relationships, monetary transactions etc.<br />I enjoyed this book a lot - if you are able to avoid spoilers, I think this book would be even better<br />Highly recommended</span>

<div id="review_102009307" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/102009307" />
    <a title="Jafar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26188-jafar"><img alt="Jafar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407873633p2/26188.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/102009307?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jafar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jafar" href="/user/show/26188-jafar">Jafar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer102009307" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2511684604167046328">Not much of a plot, but a really great idea for a novel combined with some great writing. The consciousness of a dying doctor, Tom Friendly, starts living his life backward as soon as he dies. This is not just telling the story of his life backward. Tom’s consciousness experiences his life with time literally reversed. Eating means throwing up food and putting it back on the plate. Constipation is quite a bizarre situation. Tom breaks up with his lovers, then is with them, then seduces them, the</span>
  <span id="freeText2511684604167046328" style="display:none">Not much of a plot, but a really great idea for a novel combined with some great writing. The consciousness of a dying doctor, Tom Friendly, starts living his life backward as soon as he dies. This is not just telling the story of his life backward. Tom’s consciousness experiences his life with time literally reversed. Eating means throwing up food and putting it back on the plate. Constipation is quite a bizarre situation. Tom breaks up with his lovers, then is with them, then seduces them, then meets them, then they disappear. He butchers his patients on the operation table and sends them away all bloody. Etc.<br /><br />Tom is a former Nazi officer who fled Germany after the war and is living in the U.S. with an assumed identity. He goes back to Europe to see the aftermath of the war. “After” a while he finds himself serving in Auschwitz. You then realize why Amis is telling the story of his life with time’s arrow turned backward. That’s the only way to make any sense out of the Holocaust. Jews are brought to life from the furnaces, hair is put back on their heads, gold teeth are installed in their mouths, they gain weight and look better, families are united, and eventually they’re sent to their towns. Very powerful writing here. Tom’s life continues back to his early childhood memories, all the while his alter ego never quite able to figure out what to make out of the world. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2511684604167046328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2055445945" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2055445945" />
    <a title="Nicola" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4596610-nicola"><img alt="Nicola" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291056380p2/4596610.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2055445945?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicola" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicola" href="/user/show/4596610-nicola">Nicola</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596610-nicola?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2055445945" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5964417895620259871">Life never really makes a lot of sense at the best of times. All of the philosophical ponderings on the eternal question of 'Why are we here?' haven't been able to come up with anything more convincing than 'Because'. Some people may wave other explanations such as 'The ineffable plan of God', which obviously, being ineffable means that it's not going to cut it as an explanation – if it can't<b> be </b>explained then it might as well not exist. Even if it did, which is another eternal question to some </span>
  <span id="freeText5964417895620259871" style="display:none">Life never really makes a lot of sense at the best of times. All of the philosophical ponderings on the eternal question of 'Why are we here?' haven't been able to come up with anything more convincing than 'Because'. Some people may wave other explanations such as 'The ineffable plan of God', which obviously, being ineffable means that it's not going to cut it as an explanation – if it can't<b> be </b>explained then it might as well not exist. Even if it did, which is another eternal question to some people. Some chuck out Chaos Theory and who knows whatall (possibly someone does - there might be people out there who've made it their life’s work to see what every body on the planet thinks is the meaning for our existence and the existence of life itself if they can manage to pull their heads out of their egocentric/human-centric world view long enough to acknowledge the existence and even possible importance of, species other than their own. <br /><br />I still don't think theres been a more convincing answer than 'Because.' Or perhaps 'Why not?'.<br /><br />In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23031.Time_s_Arrow" title="Time's Arrow by Martin Amis" rel="nofollow noopener">Time's Arrow</a> we see through the eyes of a man who is living backwards. We aren't him, we are a voice inside his head that he can't hear so we can only talk to ourselves and experience only that which applies to him. Which is a pain because this fellow was probably dead when we first arrived and we have a long, tedious journey back to any kind of exciting existence. 'We' are alive, but this guy is the next worst thing to a corpse. Being this old really sucks. Being this powerless is extremely frustrating. It's amazing that we don't go completely insane living inside this guys head and start hearing voices inside his head!<br /><br />We also appear to be the only one aware of the extremely bizarre state of the world. Things really don't make any sense at all. Why are we the only ones who care? Why does Dr. Todd Friendly (the man in whose head we currently live) not seem to notice that something is wrong? <br /><br />Who exactly are we again?<br /><br />I've read a few things which deal with concept of living backwards - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/98785.Backwards__Red_Dwarf__4_" title="Backwards (Red Dwarf #4) by Rob Grant" rel="nofollow noopener">Backwards</a> is the funniest and I remember a particularly poignant short story by Fritz Leiber I think where a man lived through many lifetimes watching the devolution of human civilization and the gradual disappearance of humanity itself. Each approaches the idea in ways both similar and individual. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23031.Time_s_Arrow" title="Time's Arrow by Martin Amis" rel="nofollow noopener">Time's Arrow</a> stands alone and if you find the idea intriguing then I would advise you to read it without knowing anything more about it unless it is already too late. I, alas, had had the secret of the good doctor's past revealed to me prior to reading this and will say that it definitely impacted on my enjoyment. The story stands on its own but there is that thrill of discovery on an initial read, the delight of peeling back the layers to see what lies beneath. You only get to experience that the once (unless you have a very bad memory), and I do feel rather cheated. I would have enjoyed it.<br /><br />Ah well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5964417895620259871" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_913374607" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/913374607" />
    <a title="Sentimental Surrealist" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9707921-sentimental-surrealist"><img alt="Sentimental Surrealist" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1412974239p2/9707921.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/913374607?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sentimental Surrealist" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sentimental Surrealist" href="/user/show/9707921-sentimental-surrealist">Sentimental Surrealist</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9707921-sentimental-surrealist?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9707921-sentimental-surrealist?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9707921-sentimental-surrealist?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9707921-sentimental-surrealist?shelf=file-under-disappointments">file-under-disappointments</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9707921-sentimental-surrealist?shelf=marcel-proust">marcel-proust</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer913374607" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3777472087544845458">A frustrating experience. See, I'd had Martin Amis hyped to me as one of the funniest writers of the whole goddamn 20th century; a classmate of mine referred to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18828.The_Rachel_Papers' title='The Rachel Papers by Martin Amis' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Rachel Papers</a> as the funniest book he'd read besides <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6759.Infinite_Jest' title='Infinite Jest by David Foster Wallace' rel='nofollow noopener'>Infinite Jest</a>, and anyone who knows me knows an Infinite Jest comparison is going to pique my interest. Well, Amis' style of humor may have worked for him, and maybe it's different in the Rachel Papers (being Amis' first novel, it's entirely possible), but it didn't really work her</span>
  <span id="freeText3777472087544845458" style="display:none">A frustrating experience. See, I'd had Martin Amis hyped to me as one of the funniest writers of the whole goddamn 20th century; a classmate of mine referred to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18828.The_Rachel_Papers" title="The Rachel Papers by Martin Amis" rel="nofollow noopener">The Rachel Papers</a> as the funniest book he'd read besides <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6759.Infinite_Jest" title="Infinite Jest by David Foster Wallace" rel="nofollow noopener">Infinite Jest</a>, and anyone who knows me knows an Infinite Jest comparison is going to pique my interest. Well, Amis' style of humor may have worked for him, and maybe it's different in the Rachel Papers (being Amis' first novel, it's entirely possible), but it didn't really work here. If the humor found here is any indication, Amis seems to find acting as though he's above all of his characters and flaunting his intelligence the very height of hilarity. He's also amused by poop and dick jokes, and while this is also true of me, his particular poop and dick jokes don't connect with me at all. On top of that, the famous reverse-time aspect of this novel struck me as an annoying gimmick for the first two-thirds of it; I wondered where Amis was going, and came to the conclusion that Amis had basically written a dull novel about a character he felt the need to condescend to at every imaginable turn, but flipped the chronology in hopes that no one noticed what he was doing.<br /><br />So for about a hundred pages, I felt like one of those people who hates contemporary and postmodern fiction with a passion. Generally, the opposite is the case for me, but I seriously considered setting this book down; it was only the slim length of it that kept me reading. And I'm glad I did, because once Martin gets around to his point (a point I won't reveal, because I, unlike the poorly-chosen blurbs on the back cover, don't want to ruin the experience for everyone... seriously, the Vintage International edition from '92 gives the damn thing away. Someone should've done something about this), he really hits on something. Something beautiful and terrifying and provocative and disgusting. The last third of this book is wonderful, wonderful stuff, good enough to lift the rating up from a potential one (I mean, I felt his command of language, but I was deeply, deeply irritated by the first hundred-odd pages) to a three. It's one of the worst and one of the greatest literary experiences I've ever had, mashed together. The final third of it is like reading a whole different novel, which I'd ordinarily bitch about, but this different novel is so much better than what had came before I can only be appreciative. <br /><br />So I'm not sure how highly I can recommend this, because the first hundred fifteen pages (again, assuming you're reading the Vintage International edition) can only be described, at least in my eyes, as unholy shit on wings, but when it gets where it's going, it gets so good you won't even mind the gracelessness of its transition. Lie back, think of England, and for the love of god, forget every attempt at humor he tosses out there. <br /><br />Edit, a few hours later: despite this novel's positives, I still think of it more as something I dislike than something I like. Two stars it is...</span>
  <a data-text-id="3777472087544845458" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_18168609" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18168609" />
    <a title="George" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/964960-george"><img alt="George" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18168609?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="George" class="user" itemprop="url" name="George" href="/user/show/964960-george">George</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18168609" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1595631808394488753">The premise of this book is well-recorded in earlier reviews: We start with the death of a doctor named Tod Friendly, and then move backwards through his life (much life hitting the Rewind button on a VCR while the tape was still playing). In reverse, the doctors take healthy patients and leave them sick and injured, while love affairs begin with arguments and end with shy flirtation. The key here is the defining period of Tod's life, towards which we are carried, our suspicions growing along th</span>
  <span id="freeText1595631808394488753" style="display:none">The premise of this book is well-recorded in earlier reviews: We start with the death of a doctor named Tod Friendly, and then move backwards through his life (much life hitting the Rewind button on a VCR while the tape was still playing). In reverse, the doctors take healthy patients and leave them sick and injured, while love affairs begin with arguments and end with shy flirtation. The key here is the defining period of Tod's life, towards which we are carried, our suspicions growing along the way. To me, though, the strongest parts are the minor details. For instance, Tod appreciates the game of chess, the point of which seems to be to take widely scattered figures and gradually arrange them all in neat rows, and to do so no faster or slower than the person sitting across from you. The constant rewind does create a sort of seasickness at times, most notably in the (mercifully few) pieces of dialogue, which force the reader to rearrange how the lines follow each other as you read. In less capable hands, this could have been a pointless grad school writing exercise, but Amis manages to give it a point, and an air of literary credibility.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1595631808394488753" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<ul>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bp0fsbIvaRxMvP/YjgWhqWXvG3gVEyA8ESKHToC0vo1Uu3ZjCXGgdOuXS7l58Kt2s7D8sNuerltKXMH/TfdMFw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('SmNv+TamFdoXoQz2a8LyWoXH9WXI6BcR4WtixEVAJ6hwRQYrjfjcsrCKuJecN/iFU5gSrQZlmXa6FSR1iAPVMg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

The Insult is about a man blinded by a robber in a supermarket parking lot who discovers one night that--because of a bizarre experiment--he can see again. Thomson\'s latest finds three very different characters--an aimless waitress, a reluctant hit man, and an ambitious young marketing executive--linked by the sudden success of a new soft drink. But a closer look confirms the feeling that Soft! continues the author\'s fascination with the way science can bend and shape the destinies of all sorts of nonscientific people. Certainly Glade Spencer, the flaky young woman who flies off periodically for unpleasant encounters with her American lawyer boyfriend, has no idea when she signs up for a sleep clinic to earn some extra cash that the soda slogans planted in her brain could cause her death. Barker Dodds, the nightclub bouncer from Plymouth, doesn\'t know why he\'s being paid to kill Glade. And James Lyle, the striving marketer who thought up the brainwashing scheme in the first place, is deliberately out of the loop about its consequences. All three are so perfectly drawn that you\'d recognize them on the street, and the way Thomson describes their quirky, weirdly decorated flats and lifestyles captures the flickering pulse of London           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<a href="/author/show/11337.Martin_Amis">Martin Amis

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8620984739990560518">Martin Amis is an English novelist, essayist, and short story writer. His works include the novels <i>Money</i>, <i>London Fields</i> and <i>The Information</i>.<br/><br/><i>The Guardian</i> writes that "all his critics have noted what <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13078.Kingsley_Amis' title='Kingsley Amis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Kingsley Amis</a> [his father] complained of as a 'terrible compulsive vividness in his style... that constant demonstrating of his command of English'; and it's true that the Amis-ness of Amis will be reco</span>
  <span id="freeText8620984739990560518" style="display:none">Martin Amis is an English novelist, essayist, and short story writer. His works include the novels <i>Money</i>, <i>London Fields</i> and <i>The Information</i>.<br /><br /><i>The Guardian</i> writes that "all his critics have noted what <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13078.Kingsley_Amis" title="Kingsley Amis" rel="nofollow noopener">Kingsley Amis</a> [his father] complained of as a 'terrible compulsive vividness in his style... that constant demonstrating of his command of English'; and it's true that the Amis-ness of Amis will be recognisable in any piece before he reaches his first full stop."<br /><br />Amis's raw material is what he sees as the absurdity of the postmodern condition with its grotesque caricatures. He has thus sometimes been portrayed as the undisputed master of what the <i>New York Times</i> has called "the new unpleasantness."<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8620984739990560518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

We are, as it turns out, all jerry-built. Or not even. You can just stick your head under the flap of the tent and crawl right in. If you get the okay. &rdquo;</span>
